The Inherence of Multiplicity: Two Possibilities and Their Absurdity
Sanusi enumerates two possibilities for inherence of multiplicity and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimūn argue that multiplicity cannot inhere in the many insofar as they are many, and how both whole and distributive inherence lead to contradictions.

Core Idea
Multiplicity cannot inhere in the many as many without destroying the unity of number.

This lesson unpacks the first part of a key kalam proof against the extramental existence of number. The commentator considers how multiplicity (kathra) could inhere in the many (kathīr). He eliminates the possibility that it inheres in the many as many, showing that both whole and distributive inherence lead to absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note that the author first asserts multiplicity cannot inhere in itself or in non-many, so it must inhere in the many.
This sets up the problem.
2
Understand the first possibility: inherence in the many as many
Focus on the subcase where multiplicity inheres in the many 'insofar as they are many'. The commentator then splits this into two: whole inherence or distributive inherence.
The phrase 'من حيث هو كثير' means 'insofar as it is many'.
3
Analyze the absurdity of whole inherence
If the individual one (al-wāḥid al-shakhṣī) inheres wholly in each individual, then duality would inhere in each unit, making the one two. This is known false by intuition.
The example of duality (اثنينية) illustrates this.
4
Analyze the absurdity of distributive inherence
If the number inheres distributively, then the attribute is not a single unit with individual unity, contradicting the claim that number is a real attribute.
The commentator says 'لم تكن الاثنينية صفة واحدة وحدة شخصية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : المتكلمون أنكروا العدد وجودية ( فإن قامت ) والأظهر أن يقال وإلا قامت ؛ أي الكثرة ( بالكثير) إذ لا يتصور قيامها بذاتها ولا بغير الكثير، وحينئذ ( فإما ) أن تقوم بالكثير ( من حيث هو كثير فيلزم قيام الواحد ( الشخصي ) بالكثير) فإن قام ذلك الواحد بتمامه بكل واحد من الكثير كان مما علم بطلانه بالبديهة مع استلزامه هاهنا محالاً آخر، فإن الاثنينية مثلاً، لو قامت بكل واحد من الواحدين كان الواحد اثنين وإن قام بالكثير على سبيل التوزيع بأن يقوم شيء من الاثنينية بهذا، وشيء آخر بذاك لم تكن الاثنينية صفة واحدة وحدة شخصية كما ادعيتموه ( أو ) تقوم بالكثير ) من حيث عرض له أمر صار به واحداً فننقل الكلام إليه أي إلى ذلك الأمر الذي صار به الكثير شيئاً واحداً صالحاً لأن يحل فيه واحد شخصي فنقول ذلك الأمر إما أن يحل في الكثير من حيث هو كثير وأنه اتصاف شيء بأمر اعتباري بحيثية دون أخرى بخلاف الأمور الخارجية فإنَّ الاتصاف بها حاصل مع قطع النظر عن ملاحظة العقل واعتباره . قوله : ( والأظهر إلخ ) لئلا يحتاج إلى تقدير الجزاء أي وإلا قامت بالكثير فإن قامت إلخ أو إلى تقدير الاحتمال الثاني بقوله : فإن أقامت إلخ وإن لم يقم بالكثير يلزم قيام الكثرة بذاتها أو قيامها بغير محلها كما يشعر قول الشارح قدس سره : إذ لا يتصور إلخ . قوله : ( من حيث هو كثير أي من حيث ذاته لا من حيث عروض أمر صار به واحداً وليس المراد به من حيث أنه متصف بالكثرة إذ لا معنى لعروض الكثرة لشيء من حيث إنه متصف بالكثرة . قوله : ( من حيث عرض له إلخ) أشار بقيد العروض إلى أن ذلك الأمر لا يجوز أن يكون أمراً اعتبارياً لأنَّ معروض الكثرة يكون ذات الكثرة فيعود المحذور المذكور. قوله : ( وفيه بحث لأنه مبني الخ) وإنما جعل المبني منحصراً في اتحاد الوحدة الاتصالية واتصال الجسم، لأن تلازمهما لا يفيد وجودها كما ظن، لأن المذكور فيما سبق وجود اتصال الجسم، ويجوز على تقدير مغايرته للوحدة الاتصالية أن تكون هي أمراً اعتبارياً لازماً لذلك الأمر الوجودي . قوله : ( لم تكن الاثنينية صفة واحدة وحدة شخصية فإن قلت : الانقسام بحسب المحل لا ينافي الوحدة الشخصية كما لا ينافي انقسام زيد بحسب الأجزاء وحدته الشخصية فإن السواد القائم بهذا الجسم واحد وحدة شخصية، وإن كان منقسماً قلت : المحل إذا كان منفصلاً بعضه عن بعض بأن يكون أحدهما في المشرق والآخر في المغرب مثلاً . فادعاء أن العرض الموجود القائم بهما على الانقسام واحد بالهوية كادعاء أن زيداً الموجود في المشرق وعمراً الموجود في المغرب واحد بالهوية فلا يلتفت إليه فتأمل قوله : ( فتنقل الكلام إليه أي إلى ذلك الأمر قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الأمر اعتبارياً الكثرة التي هي باطل أو من حيث عرض له ما به صار واحداً ) ويلزم التسلسل فوجب أن تكون العدد أمراً اعتبارياً وهو المطلوب.
The Second Observatory - Fifth Purpose: The theologians denied number. Existent (if it inheres) and the more apparent is to say, else it inheres; that is, multiplicity (in the many) since it is inconceivable that it inheres in itself or in other than the many. Then (so it is) either that it inheres in the many (insofar as it is many, necessitating the inherence of the individual (singular) one in the many). For if that one inheres in its entirety in each individual of the many, that is known to be false by intuition, and it entails here another impossibility. For example, duality: if it inhered in each of the two ones, the one would be two. And if it inheres in the many by distribution, such that a part of duality inheres in this and another part in that, then duality would not be a single attribute with individual unity as you claim. (Or) it inheres in the many (by virtue of an accident that made it one, so we transfer the discussion to that accident which made the many one thing suitable for an individual one to inhere in it, and we say that accident either inheres in the many insofar as it is many, and that is the description of a thing by a conceptual matter in a certain respect and not another, unlike external matters, for their description occurs regardless of the intellect's consideration and its regard. His statement: (And the more apparent, etc.) is to avoid needing to estimate the apodosis, i.e., 'else it inheres in the many, and if it inheres etc.' or to estimate the second possibility by his statement: 'for if it inheres etc., and if it does not inhere in the many, it necessitates the inherence of multiplicity in itself or its inherence in something other than its locus, as is suggested by the commentator's statement, may his secret be sanctified: 'since it is inconceivable etc.' His statement: ('insofar as it is many') means insofar as its essence, not by virtue of an accident that made it one, and it is not intended that it is described by multiplicity, for there is no meaning to the accident of multiplicity for something insofar as it is described by multiplicity. His statement: ('by virtue of an accident that made it one, etc.') He indicated by the qualification of accident that that matter cannot be conceptual, because the subject of multiplicity would be the essence of multiplicity, and the aforementioned absurdity recurs. His statement: (And there is a discussion because it is based, etc.) He made the basis restricted to the union of the connective unity and the connection of the body, because their mutual entailment does not imply its existence as thought, for what was mentioned previously is the existence of the connection of the body, and it is possible on the assumption of its difference from the connective unity that it be a conceptual matter necessary for that existential matter. His statement: (Duality would not be a single attribute with individual unity. If you say: division according to the locus does not contradict individual unity, just as the division of Zayd according to parts does not contradict his individual unity, for the blackness inherent in this body is one with individual unity even if it is divided, I say: the locus if it is separated part from part, such that one is in the east and the other in the west, for example, then the claim that the accident existing inhering in both by division is one in identity is like the claim that Zayd existing in the east and ʿAmr existing in the west are one in identity, so no attention is paid to it. So reflect on his statement: (So we transfer the discussion to it, i.e., to that matter. It was said: why is it not permissible that that matter be conceptual, the multiplicity that is false, or by virtue of an accident that made it one?) and infinite regress follows, so it is necessary that number be conceptual, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) that number (ʿadad) is not an existent entity but a conceptual (iʿtibārī) matter. The argument proceeds by analyzing how multiplicity (kathra) could inhere (qiyām) in the many (kathīr). The commentator first notes that multiplicity cannot inhere in itself or in something other than the many, so it must inhere in the many. Then he considers two possibilities: either it inheres in the many insofar as they are many, or it inheres after the many become one through some accident. The first possibility leads to absurdity: if the individual unity inheres wholly in each individual, then each individual would be that unity, making the one two (e.g., duality in each one makes the one two). If it inheres distributively, then the unity is not a single individual attribute. The second possibility transfers the problem to the accident that makes the many one. That accident must itself inhere either in the many as many (leading to the same absurdity) or in a further accident, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Therefore, number must be conceptual. The commentary explains nuances: the 'individual one' (al-wāḥid al-shakhṣī) is the specific unity of a quality; the division by locus does not negate unity if the locus is contiguous, but if the locus is separated (e.g., east and west), the unity cannot be real. Thus the argument concludes that number is a conceptual entity, which is the desired claim of the theologians who deny the real existence of number.

Key Terms
عدد
كثرة
واحد شخصي
قيام
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the absurdity of the whole inherence of the individual one in each of the many?
A) It leads to infinite regress.
B) It makes the one two (e.g., duality in each one).
C) It contradicts the principle of non-contradiction.
D) It requires the many to be one.

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity cannot inhere in the many as many because both whole and distributive inherence destroy the unity of number.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that even a simple concept like 'two' cannot be a real attribute without absurdity. Have you ever treated an abstract concept (like 'success' or 'status') as if it had independent reality, thereby subtly associating partners with Allah in your mind? Examine one such concept today and consciously relegate it to a mere effect of Allah's will.
?
Sanusi uses rigorous logic to defend tawhid. Can you defend your belief in the oneness of Allah with similar precision? If someone pressed you with a logical objection, would you falter? Commit to learning the rational proofs of tawhid so that your faith is not merely inherited but intellectually certain.
?
The inherence of multiplicity in the many leads to absurdity. Reflect on your own life: have you allowed multiplicity to creep into your worship—dividing your heart among multiple loyalties, distractions, or priorities? What one worldly attachment can you sacrifice today to restore the unity of purpose in your devotion to Allah?

The Infinite Regress Argument and the Conceptual Nature of Number
Sanusi presents the second possibility and the infinite regress argument, concluding that number is conceptual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the alternative possibility—that multiplicity inheres after the many become one through an accident—leads to infinite regress, compelling the conclusion that number is conceptual.

Core Idea
If multiplicity inheres in the many after they become one, that unifying accident itself requires another accident, leading to an infinite chain.

This lesson completes the proof by examining the second possibility: that multiplicity inheres in the many not as many but after they are unified by an accident. The argument transfers the problem to that accident, which either inheres as many (repeating the absurdity) or requires another accident, leading to infinite regress. Hence, number must be conceptual (iʿtibārī).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the transition
Read the Arabic passage, noting that after rejecting the first possibility, the author introduces the second: multiplicity inheres in the many after an accident makes them one. The discussion is transferred to that accident.
Look for the phrase 'أو تقوم بالكثير من حيث عرض له أمر صار به واحداً'.
2
Understand the transfer of discussion
The author says: 'we transfer the discussion to that accident' (فننقل الكلام إليه). This accident is supposed to make the many one and thus a suitable locus for the individual one.
3
Analyze the two sub-possibilities for the accident
The accident either inheres in the many as many (leading to the same absurdity as before) or it requires another accident to unify it, leading to infinite regress.
The commentator adds that the accident cannot be conceptual because then the same problem recurs.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since both paths lead to absurdity, the only remaining option is that number is a conceptual (iʿtibārī) entity, not an extramental existent. This is the desired conclusion for the theologians.
The last line: 'ويلزم التسلسل فوجب أن تكون العدد أمراً اعتبارياً وهو المطلوب.'

The Second Observatory - Fifth Purpose: The theologians denied number. Existent (if it inheres) and the more apparent is to say, else it inheres; that is, multiplicity (in the many) since it is inconceivable that it inheres in itself or in other than the many. Then (so it is) either that it inheres in the many (insofar as it is many, necessitating the inherence of the individual (singular) one in the many). For if that one inheres in its entirety in each individual of the many, that is known to be false by intuition, and it entails here another impossibility. For example, duality: if it inhered in each of the two ones, the one would be two. And if it inheres in the many by distribution, such that a part of duality inheres in this and another part in that, then duality would not be a single attribute with individual unity as you claim. (Or) it inheres in the many (by virtue of an accident that made it one, so we transfer the discussion to that accident which made the many one thing suitable for an individual one to inhere in it, and we say that accident either inheres in the many insofar as it is many, and that is the description of a thing by a conceptual matter in a certain respect and not another, unlike external matters, for their description occurs regardless of the intellect's consideration and its regard. His statement: (And the more apparent, etc.) is to avoid needing to estimate the apodosis, i.e., 'else it inheres in the many, and if it inheres etc.' or to estimate the second possibility by his statement: 'for if it inheres etc., and if it does not inhere in the many, it necessitates the inherence of multiplicity in itself or its inherence in something other than its locus, as is suggested by the commentator's statement, may his secret be sanctified: 'since it is inconceivable etc.' His statement: ('insofar as it is many') means insofar as its essence, not by virtue of an accident that made it one, and it is not intended that it is described by multiplicity, for there is no meaning to the accident of multiplicity for something insofar as it is described by multiplicity. His statement: ('by virtue of an accident that made it one, etc.') He indicated by the qualification of accident that that matter cannot be conceptual, because the subject of multiplicity would be the essence of multiplicity, and the aforementioned absurdity recurs. His statement: (And there is a discussion because it is based, etc.) He made the basis restricted to the union of the connective unity and the connection of the body, because their mutual entailment does not imply its existence as thought, for what was mentioned previously is the existence of the connection of the body, and it is possible on the assumption of its difference from the connective unity that it be a conceptual matter necessary for that existential matter. His statement: (Duality would not be a single attribute with individual unity. If you say: division according to the locus does not contradict individual unity, just as the division of Zayd according to parts does not contradict his individual unity, for the blackness inherent in this body is one with individual unity even if it is divided, I say: the locus if it is separated part from part, such that one is in the east and the other in the west, for example, then the claim that the accident existing inhering in both by division is one in identity is like the claim that Zayd existing in the east and ʿAmr existing in the west are one in identity, so no attention is paid to it. So reflect on his statement: (So we transfer the discussion to it, i.e., to that matter. It was said: why is it not permissible that that matter be conceptual, the multiplicity that is false, or by virtue of an accident that made it one?) and infinite regress follows, so it is necessary that number be conceptual, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) that number (ʿadad) is not an existent entity but a conceptual (iʿtibārī) matter. The argument proceeds by analyzing how multiplicity (kathra) could inhere (qiyām) in the many (kathīr). The commentator first notes that multiplicity cannot inhere in itself or in something other than the many, so it must inhere in the many. Then he considers two possibilities: either it inheres in the many insofar as they are many, or it inheres after the many become one through some accident. The first possibility leads to absurdity: if the individual unity inheres wholly in each individual, then each individual would be that unity, making the one two (e.g., duality in each one makes the one two). If it inheres distributively, then the unity is not a single individual attribute. The second possibility transfers the problem to the accident that makes the many one. That accident must itself inhere either in the many as many (leading to the same absurdity) or in a further accident, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Therefore, number must be conceptual. The commentary explains nuances: the 'individual one' (al-wāḥid al-shakhṣī) is the specific unity of a quality; the division by locus does not negate unity if the locus is contiguous, but if the locus is separated (e.g., east and west), the unity cannot be real. Thus the argument concludes that number is a conceptual entity, which is the desired claim of the theologians who deny the real existence of number.

Misconception: One might think that infinite regress is a purely abstract logical puzzle without theological consequences.
Correction: The passage shows that infinite regress directly leads to the conclusion that number is conceptual, undermining the realist ontology of number.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the final conclusion about the nature of number?
A) Number is a real attribute inherent in things.
B) Number is a conceptual (iʿtibārī) entity.
C) Number is impossible to define.
D) Number is created by God.

Key Takeaway
The only way to avoid absurdity is to affirm that number is a conceptual entity, not an extramental existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on numbers every day—from counting prayers to calculating inheritance. When you learned that number is merely conceptual, not an extramental reality, did it shake your trust in the objectivity of those numbers? How does this affect your certainty in matters where numbers are used as proof?
?
The argument here uses infinite regress to show that if unity were not merely a mental concept, an endless chain of accidents would follow. Consider the infinite possibilities in your own life—regrets, plans, anxieties. Are you carrying an infinite regress in your heart that keeps you from seeing the conceptual nature of your worries? Can you let go of that chain and affirm that only Allah has true extramental existence?

The Equivocal Meanings of Unity and the Existence of Multiplicity
The commentator analyzes the equivocal meanings of unity and their implications for the existence of multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that unity (al-wāḥid) is used equivocally for existential unity (by connection/union) and conceptual unity (absence of division), and that multiplicity follows the ontological status of its constituent units.

Core Idea
Unity is not always existential; multiplicity reflects the reality of its units.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif investigates the nature of unity and its relation to multiplicity. The commentator clarifies that the term 'one' can refer to different concepts: the existential unity we perceive in connected bodies, and the conceptual unity of indivisibility. This distinction is crucial for understanding whether numbers have real existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Note that the passage includes a discussion of the equivocal meanings of 'one' and a subsequent analysis of multiplicity.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'one'
The passage states that 'one' is said by equivocation. Identify the two main meanings: one by connection/union (existential) and one as absence of division (conceptual).
The first is considered existential because we witness connected bodies; the second is conceptual because it involves non-existence.
3
Understand the principle of multiplicity following units
The author argues that multiplicity is nothing but the aggregate of units, so it follows the units in existence. If units are existential, multiplicity is existential; if units are conceptual, multiplicity is conceptual.
This leads to a nuanced position: not all numbers are existent nor all non-existent; it depends on the type of unity.
4
Consider the objection and its resolution
The passage presents an objection that number is existent because it inheres in existing numbered things. The commentator responds by noting that the conceptual nature of unity does not negate the transfer of discussion, and that the infinite regress stops with cessation of consideration.
This strengthens the distinction between existential and conceptual unity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ الواحد كما علمته يقال بالتشكيك على معان؛ كالواحد بالاتصال والاجتماع ووحدته أمر وجودي بالضرورة ) لأنا نشاهد اتصال الأجسام واجتماعها وقد يقال : إن المشاهد هو المتصل والمجتمع وليسا نفس الوحدة وأما الاتصال والاجتماع فلا نسلم كونهما موجودين فضلا أن يكونا مشاهدين، وشهادة الحس باتصاف الجسم بهما لا تدل على مشاهدتهما كما في الاتصاف بالعمى هذا إن جعل الوحدة نفس الاتصال والاجتماع وإن جعلت كما هو الحق عبارة عن عدم الانقسام العارض للمتصل والمجتمع باعتبار الاتصال والاجتماع كانت أمراً اعتبارياً كما صرح به في قوله ( وككونه لا ينقسم إذ ليس له كم يفرض فيه شيء غير شيء وأنه اعتباري) لأن العدم مأخوذ فيه ( والكثرة ليست إلا مجموع الوحدات فهي تتبعها في الوجود) فإن كانت الوحدات موجودة كالوحدات الاتصالية والاجتماعية كانت الكثرة المركبة منها موجودة أيضاً. إذ ليس لها جزء سوى تلك الوحدات الموجودة وإن كانت الوحدات أموراً معدومة كالوحدات بمعنى اللا انقسامات كانت الكثرة المركبة منها معدومة أيضاً وحينئذ لا يصح أن يقال : إِنَّ كل عدد موجود ولا أنه لا شيء من العدد بموجود بل الحق هو التفصيل وفيه بحث على أن الاتصال والاجتماع نفس الوحدة مع كونهما وجوديين والصواب أنهما سببان لعروض الوحدة الاعتبارية كما أشرنا إليه ثم إن هاهنا معارضة دالة على أن الكثرة موجودة وهي أن يقال : إِنَّ العدد أمر واحد قائم بالمعدودات الموجودة قال لأنه مبني قوله : ( واعلم إلخ ) تحقيق للمقام ومحاكمة من غير تراضي الخصمين . قوله : (إذ ليس له كم متعلق بلا ينقسم . قوله : ( إذ ليس لها جزء إلخ ) حتى يمكن أن يكون عدمها بعدم ذلك الجزء . قوله : ( وحينئذ أي إذا كانت الوحدة منقسمة إلى الوجودية والعدمية . فإن قلت : الاعتباري لا ينافي نقل الكلام قلت: أولاً منقوض بالوحدة الاعتبارية، وثانياً ينقطع التسلسل بانقطاع الاعتبار اللهم إلا أن يقال : لا يكفي عروض الأمر الاعتباري في قيام الكثرة الموجودة في الخارج وفيه تأمل. يتم . قوله : ( هذا إن جعل الوحدة الخ) أي كون الوحدة أمراً وجودياً كما قال المصنف وإن لم قوله: (وككونه في عطفه على كالواحد مسامحة ظاهرة وجعل الكون بمعنى الكائن يأباه إضافته إلى الضمير.
Know that the one, as you have learned, is said by equivocation (tashkīk) for meanings: like the one by connection (ittiṣāl) and union (ijtimāʿ), and its unity is an existential matter necessarily, for we witness the connection of bodies and their union. And it may be said: what is witnessed is the connected and the united, not the unity itself; as for connection and union, we do not concede that they are existent, let alone that they are witnessed. The testimony of sense to the body's being described by them does not indicate that they are witnessed, as in the case of being described by blindness. This is if unity is made identical to connection and union. And if it is made, as is the truth, an expression of the absence of division (ʿadam al-inqisām) that accrues to the connected and united by virtue of connection and union, then it is a conceptual matter, as stated in his statement: (And its being that it is not divisible, because it has no quantity (kamm) in which something other than something can be supposed, and that it is conceptual) because non-existence (ʿadam) is taken in it. (And multiplicity is nothing but the aggregate of units, so it follows them in existence.) For if the units are existent, like the connective and social units, then the multiplicity composed of them is also existent, since it has no part other than those existent units. And if the units are non-existent things, like the units in the sense of non-divisions, then the multiplicity composed of them is also non-existent. In that case, it is not correct to say that every number is existent, nor that no number is existent; rather the truth is the distinction. And there is discussion about that connection and union being the same unity while being existential, and the correct view is that they are causes for the accrual of conceptual unity, as we indicated. Then there is here an objection indicating that multiplicity is existent, which is to say: number is a single thing inherent in the numbered things that are existent. He said: because it is based. His statement: (Know, etc.) is an investigation of the station and a judgment without the two opponents' consent. His statement: (Because it has no quantity) is connected to 'is not divisible.' His statement: (Because it has no part, etc.) so that its non-existence could be by the non-existence of that part. His statement: (In that case) i.e., when unity is divided into existential and non-existent. Then if you say: the conceptual does not negate the transfer of speech, I say: first, it is invalidated by conceptual unity, and second, the infinite regress stops with the cessation of consideration, unless it is said: the accrual of the conceptual matter is not sufficient for the inherence of existent multiplicity in the external, and there is reflection in it. It is completed. His statement: (This is if unity is made, etc.) i.e., that unity is an existential thing as the author said, even if not. His statement: (And its being, in its conjunction with 'like the one' is a clear looseness, and making 'being' in the sense of 'existent' is prevented by its addition to the pronoun.)
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a discussion and analysis (taḥqīq) of the nature of unity (al-wāḥid) and its implications for the existence of number. The author first notes that the term 'one' is used equivocally (tashkīk) for different meanings: one by connection (ittiṣāl) and union (ijtimāʿ), and one as the absence of division (ʿadam al-inqisām). The former is considered existential (wujūdī) because we observe connected and united bodies, but the commentator objects that what we observe is the connected body, not the connection itself. The latter is conceptual (iʿtibārī) because it involves non-existence. The author then argues that multiplicity (kathra) follows the units: if the units are existential, multiplicity is existential; if the units are conceptual, multiplicity is conceptual. Thus, the correct view is that not all numbers are existent, nor are they all non-existent; it depends on the type of unity. The passage then presents an objection (muʿāraḍa) that number must be existent because it is a single thing inhering in numbered objects. The commentator responds by analyzing the objection and pointing out that the conceptual nature of unity does not prevent the transfer of discussion; rather, it stops the infinite regress. The passage concludes with further clarifications on the distinction between existential and conceptual unity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All unity is existential because we perceive connected objects.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between the perceived connected object and the concept of unity itself; unity can be conceptual (absence of division) even when we perceive connected things.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is multiplicity considered existent?
A) When the units are existential, like connective units.
B) When the units are non-existent, like absences of division.
C) Always, because number is a single thing inherent in numbered objects.
D) Never, because number is purely conceptual.

Key Takeaway
Unity can be existential (by connection) or conceptual (absence of division), and multiplicity inherits the ontological status of its constituent units.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you assume that the 'unity' you feel—like the unity of your goals or your community—is real and existential, or have you considered that it might be merely conceptual, a mental construct that could disappear if you examine it closely? What would change if you found it was only conceptual?
?
You believe in the oneness of Allah (tawḥīd). But reflect: is your understanding of that unity existential (like a number one) or conceptual (like absence of division)? The passage shows that not all unity is the same. How does this distinction affect the way you think about God's oneness? Be honest: have you ever thought of Allah's unity as a kind of 'number one'?
?
The passage argues that multiplicity follows its units. Think of a 'multiplicity' in your life—like your many responsibilities or relationships. Are the 'units' (individual tasks or people) truly existent in a way that makes the whole real, or are some of them just conceptual? Your time is finite; which units are merely conceptual and draining you? What are you going to do about it today?

Objection and Reply on Mental Consideration
Al-Jurjānī answers an objection about mental consideration and regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjānī defends the role of mental consideration in stopping regress arguments.

Core Idea
Mental consideration can stop regress because the act of considering is finite.

Al-Jurjānī now addresses a key objection: if number is merely a mental consideration, why can't we apply the regress argument against it? He provides two clear replies and notes a further subtlety. This lesson examines his defence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall dialectic: objection, two replies, and a final remark.
Notice the structure: 'فإن قلت ... قلت أولاً ... وثانياً ... اللهم إلا أن يقال ...'
2
Identify the two replies
Find the first reply (by analogy with unity) and the second reply (regress stops with cessation of consideration). Understand how each works.
The first is a tuqūḍ (counterexample), the second is a direct argument.
3
Reflect on the nuance
Consider the final remark about whether mental consideration is sufficient for external multiplicity. Why does the author say 'fīhi taʾammul'?
This shows scholarly caution – not every point is settled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: الاعتباري لا ينافي نقل الكلام قلت: أولاً منقوض بالوحدة الاعتبارية، وثانياً ينقطع التسلسل بانقطاع الاعتبار اللهم إلا أن يقال لا يكفي عروض الأمر الاعتباري في قيام الكثرة الموجودة في الخارج وفيه تأمل.
If you say: 'The mental consideration does not prevent the transmission of discourse [regress]?' I say: First, it is invalidated by the mental consideration of unity; second, the regress is stopped by the cessation of consideration—unless it is said that the occurrence of the mental consideration is not sufficient for the subsistence of multiplicity that is existent in the external world; but this [objection] deserves consideration.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage records an objection-and-answer pair within the discussion. The opponent argues that even if number is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī), that does not block the regress argument (naql al-kalām). The author gives two replies: First, the objection is inconsistent because unity itself is also considered mental and yet accepted. Second, the regress terminates when the act of consideration stops—meaning the mental positing of units has a limit, so no infinite regress. A further possible objection (that mental consideration cannot ground external multiplicity) is noted as requiring further thought (fīhi taʾammul), acknowledging the nuance.

Key Terms
اعتباري
نقل الكلام
التسلسل
الوحدة الاعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mental considerations cannot stop a regress; the author shows they can by the very nature of consideration being finite.
Correction: Mental consideration is an act that can be terminated, and when it ceases, the regress stops.

Checkpoint
What is the second reply to the objection that mental consideration does not prevent regress?
A) The regress actually continues without end.
B) The regress is stopped by the cessation of the mental consideration.
C) The regress is stopped by the existence of external units.
D) The regress is only apparent, not real.

Key Takeaway
Mental consideration, being finite in act, can terminate regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself trapped in an endless loop of doubts or justifications about your faith? How does the idea that consideration itself is finite give you a way to stop that regress? Will you consciously halt that cycle today?
?
The first reply exposes an inconsistency in the opponent's position. Are there beliefs you hold that are inconsistent with each other? Will you take a moment to examine them and resolve the contradiction?
?
The passage suggests that mental consideration, though not real in an external sense, still holds logical weight. Do you sometimes dismiss your own intellectual considerations as 'mere thoughts' and thus avoid taking them seriously? How might that be preventing you from reaching truth?

The Impossibility of Number Subsisting in External Objects
Author presents reductio against external number

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument against Ibn Sina's claim that numbers have external existence as a single entity subsisting in multiple enumerated objects.

Core Idea
Number cannot be a single real entity subsisting in two things—it must be a mental consideration.

In this passage, the author directly engages with Ibn Sina's argument that numbers exist both in the mind and in external things. He uses a logical reductio to show that if number were a single entity subsisting in an aggregate, it would lead to absurdities. The two examples he gives—the existent and non-existent, and two persons in different locations—demonstrate the impossibility of a single identical meaning inhering in multiple subjects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: Ibn Sina's claim, then the author's rebuttal.
The author quotes Ibn Sina before responding.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's position
Ibn Sina claims number has existence in things (external) and in the soul. He denies that number has no existence except in the soul.
He concedes number cannot be separate from counted things.
3
Understand the first example
The author asks you to contemplate an existent and a non-existent—they are two, but no single existent entity subsists in them as a pair.
This shows duality cannot be an external existent.
4
Understand the second example
Two persons in different locations are two, but no single identical meaning subsists in them—only a meaning with multiplicity is possible.
This strengthens the argument against a single external number.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the thing subsisting in enumerated objects is merely a supposition and mental consideration (iʿtibārī).
This directly refutes Ibn Sina's claim of external existence.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : المتكلمون أنكروا العدد
ابن سينا : إنَّ العدد له وجود في الأشياء ووجود في النفس، ولا اعتداد بقول من قال : لا وجود له إلا في النفس نعم لو قال : لا وجود له مجردا عن المعدودات التي هي في الأعيان إلا في النفس لكان حقاً فإنه لا يتجرد عنها قائماً بنفسه، وأما أن في الموجودات أعداداً فذلك أمر لا شك فيه ولما ثبت وجود العدد ثبت وجود الوحدة المقومة له فأشار المصنف رحمه الله إلى دفع هذه المعارضة بقوله : (وأما إن أمراً واحداً يقوم بالمجموع الذي هو المعدودات ( فإن تخيل ) لم يكن ذلك الأمر واحداً موجوداً، بل ) كان اعتبارياً ضرورة أنَّ الاثنين لا يقوم بهما أمر ) موجود ) واحد بالهوية وإن شئت ) زيادة استيقان لما ذكرناه ( فاستبصر بموجود في الخارج ومعدوم فيه ) فإنهما اثنان أي الاثنينية قائمة بهما وحينئذ فلا يتصور كونها أمراً موجوداً فضلاً عن كونها واحدة بالهوية ( أو ) استبصر ( بشخص) موجود ( في الشرق و) بشخص (آخر) موجود ( في المغرب فإنهما ) أيضاً (اثنان ) أي معروضان للاثنينية ( ويعلم بالضرورة أنه لم يقم بهما معنى واحد ( بالهوية وإن أمكن أن يقوم بهذين الاثنين الموجودين معنى موجود فيه تعدد بخلاف الاثنين الأولين إذ لا يمكن أن يقوم بهما أمر موجود أصلا كما ذكرناه ( بل ذلك ) الأمر القائم بالمعدودات (مجرد) فرض و اعتبار أي أمر فرضي

قوله : ( وجود في الأشياء ) أي وجود خارجي بقرينة المقابلة .
قوله : ( فإنه لا يتجرد إلخ إذ الوحدة لا تتجرد قائمة بنفسها . قوله : ( وأما إن أمراً واحداً (الخ ما ذكره المصنف يدل على امتناع قيام العدد بالمعدود قياماً عينياً تحقيقياً، كقيام السواد لا قياماً انتزاعياً كقيام العمى بزيد على ما في الشفاء حيث وقع فيه، وأما أنَّ في الوجود أعداداً فذلك أمر لا شك فيه إذا كان في الموجودات وحدات فوق وحدة وكل واحد من الأعداد فإنه نوع بنفسه، وهو واحد في نفسه من حيث هو ذلك النوع وله من حيث هو ذلك النوع خواص والشيء الذي لا حقيقة له محال أن يكون له خاصية الأولية أو التركيب أو التمامية أو الزائدية أو الناقصية أو المربعية أو المكعبية أو الصمم وسائر الأشكال التي لها. وتلك الحقيقة وحدته التي هو بها هو انتهى . فقوله : وتلك الحقيقة وحدته التي هو بها هو صريح في أن وحدته النوعية بلوغه تلك المرتبة، وحينئذ لا استحالة في قيام العدد بالمجموع فقوله : ضرورة أن الاثنين لا يقوم بهما أمر موجود واحد بالهوية ممنوع إنما ذلك في الواحد بالهوية الذي لا يكون فيه تركب .
هي
قوله: (فاستبصر بموجود إلخ) هذا الاستبصار إنما يدل على أنَّ العدد القائم بمثل هذا المعدود لا يكون أمراً موجوداً في الخارج وذلك لا يستلزم أن لا يكون العدد القائم بالموجودات أمراً موجوداً وأما المثال الثاني فلانسلم عدم قيام معنى واحد بهما لما عرفت من معنى وحدة العدد . قوله : ( مجرد فرض و اعتبار يخدشه ما ذكره الشيخ من أنه كيف يكون لما لا حقيقة لا
خواص تترتب عليه الأحكام.

و اعتباري وإن كانت المعدودات الخارجية متصفة به فإنَّ اتصاف الموجودات العينية بالأمور الاعتبارية جائزة، وبهذا تنحل الشبهة وتنحسم مادتها فإنَّ الأعيان متصفة بالعدد بلا شك وإما أنَّ العدد العارض لها موجود خارجي فليس مما لا شك فيه وكذا الحال في الوحدة العارضة للموجود العيني .
Second Observatory - Fifth Discourse: The Mutakallimun Denied Number. Ibn Sina said: Number has existence in things and existence in the soul, and no attention is paid to the one who said: It has no existence except in the soul. Yes, if he said: It has no existence separate from the counted things which are in external objects except in the soul, that would be true, because it does not become separate from them subsisting by itself. But that numbers exist in existing things is a matter of which there is no doubt. And since the existence of number is established, the existence of the unit (al-waḥda) constituting it is established. So the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated the rebuttal of this objection by his saying: (But as for one thing subsisting in the aggregate which is the enumerated objects (then imagine) that thing is not one existent, but) it is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī), necessarily because two does not have subsisting in it an existent one in identity. And if you wish for greater certainty of what we mentioned, then contemplate an existent in the external and a non-existent in it, for they are two, i.e., duality (al-ithnayniyya) subsists in them. Then it is inconceivable that it be an existent thing, let alone being one in identity. (Or) contemplate a person existing in the east and another person existing in the west, for they are also two, i.e., subjects of duality. And it is known by necessity that a single meaning (one in identity) has not subsisted in them, although it is possible that an existent meaning exhibiting multiplicity subsists in these two existing things, unlike the first two, because no existent thing can subsist in them at all as we mentioned. (But that) thing subsisting in the enumerated objects is (merely) a supposition and a mental consideration, i.e., a suppositional matter. Commentary (qāla): (Existence in things) i.e., external existence, by virtue of the contrast. Commentary: (For it does not become separate, etc.) because unity does not become separate subsisting by itself. Commentary: (But as for one thing, etc.) What the author mentioned indicates the impossibility of number subsisting in the enumerated object in a real essential subsistence (qiyāman ʿayniyyan taḥqīqiyyan), like the subsistence of blackness, not in an abstractive subsistence (qiyāman intizāʿiyyan) like the subsistence of blindness in Zayd, as occurs in the Shifā. But that numbers exist in existence is an indisputable matter if in existing things there are unities beyond unity. And every one of the numbers is a species by itself, and it is one in itself insofar as it is that species, and it has, insofar as it is that species, properties. And a thing which has no reality is impossible to have the property of primariness, composition, completeness, increase, deficiency, squareness, cubeness, irrationality, and the rest of the forms which belong to it. And that reality is its unity by which it is what it is, end. So his saying: 'And that reality is its unity by which it is what it is' is explicit that its specific unity is its attaining that rank. And then there is no impossibility in number subsisting in the aggregate. So his saying: 'Necessarily two does not have subsisting in it an existent one in identity' is denied. That is only so for the one in identity which does not have composition. Commentary: (Contemplate an existent, etc.) This contemplation only indicates that the number subsisting in such an enumerated object is not an existent thing externally, and that does not entail that the number subsisting in existing things is not an existent thing. As for the second example, we do not concede the non-subsistence of a single meaning in them, due to what you know of the meaning of the unity of number. Commentary: (Mere supposition and mental consideration) The statement of the Shaykh that 'how can that which has no reality have properties upon which rulings depend?' detracts from it. And it is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī), even if external enumerated things are described by it, because the description of external existents by mental considerations (al-umūr al-iʿtibāriyya) is permissible. And by this the doubt is resolved and its material is cut off. For external objects are undoubtedly described by number. But that the number attaching to them is an external existent is not beyond doubt. And similarly for the unity attaching to the external existent.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Ibn Sina's claim that numbers have external existence. The author presents Ibn Sina's argument, then the author's rebuttal, followed by commentary. The argument structure: Ibn Sina says numbers exist both in things (externally) and in the mind, and that the unit (one) exists as a constituent of number. The author responds with a reductio: if number were a single entity subsisting in multiple enumerated things, then impossible consequences follow. He gives two examples: (1) an existent and a non-existent cannot have a single identical subsisting entity making them two; (2) two persons in different locations cannot have a single identical meaning subsisting in them. Therefore, number is merely a mental consideration (iʿtibārī), not an external reality. The commentator clarifies that the author means number cannot subsist in enumerated objects as a real inherent quality (like blackness), but only as a mental abstraction. The commentator also notes that numbers as types have real properties, but that does not entail external existence of the number as an accident. The passage concludes that external objects are described by number, but number itself is not an external existent—only a mental consideration, and that is sufficient.

Key Terms
عدد
وحدة
اعتباري
وجود خارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny the existence of numbers altogether.
Correction: They only deny their existence as external entities; they affirm numbers as mental considerations.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the status of number when considered in relation to external enumerated objects?
A) It is a real external entity.
B) It is a mental consideration only.
C) It is a divine attribute.
D) It is neither external nor mental.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that number cannot be a real external entity subsisting in enumerated objects; it is a mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The example of an existent and a non-existent being 'two' shows that 'twoness' can apply even when one component is nothing. How often do you assume that numbers correspond to real external things? Will you now pause and remember that even the number 'one' when applied to yourself is a mental consideration, and that true Oneness belongs only to Allah?
?
If number subsisting in objects is merely a mental consideration, then what about your judgments of 'more' and 'less'—are they also mental constructs? How does this realization affect your contentment with what you have? Will you seek to measure your life by reality rather than by numerical comparisons?

Number as a Mental Consideration: Commentary and Resolution
Commentary clarifies subsistence types and resolves objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentary's clarification that number subsists in objects abstractively (not really) and how the properties of numbers do not necessitate their external existence.

Core Idea
Number subsists in objects abstractively, like blindness in a person, not like blackness in a body.

The commentator builds on the author's argument, clarifying that number cannot subsist in enumerated objects as a real inherent quality (qiyām ʿaynī) but only as an abstractive quality (qiyām intizāʿī). He also addresses the objection that numbers have real properties, showing that these properties pertain to the number as a type, not as an external accident. The resolution is that external objects are described by number, but number itself is a mental consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic commentary once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the commentator explains the author's position.
The commentary is dense; take your time.
2
Distinguish subsistence types
The commentator explains that number cannot subsist in a real essential way (qiyām ʿaynī) like blackness, but only in an abstractive way (qiyām intizāʿī) like blindness.
This distinction is key to understanding the argument.
3
Understand the objection about properties
Ibn Sina objects that numbers have properties (like squareness, cubeness) which cannot belong to something unreal. The commentator responds that these properties belong to the number as a type, not as an accident subsisting in objects.
The number as a species has its own reality, but that does not make it an external accident.
4
See the final resolution
The commentator concludes that number is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī), even though external objects are described by number. This resolves the doubt.
Describing objects by a mental consideration is permissible.

Commentary: (Mere supposition and mental consideration) The statement of the Shaykh that 'how can that which has no reality have properties upon which rulings depend?' detracts from it. And it is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī), even if external enumerated things are described by it, because the description of external existents by mental considerations (al-umūr al-iʿtibāriyya) is permissible. And by this the doubt is resolved and its material is cut off. For external objects are undoubtedly described by number. But that the number attaching to them is an external existent is not beyond doubt. And similarly for the unity attaching to the external existent.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Ibn Sina's claim that numbers have external existence. The author presents Ibn Sina's argument, then the author's rebuttal, followed by commentary. The argument structure: Ibn Sina says numbers exist both in things (externally) and in the mind, and that the unit (one) exists as a constituent of number. The author responds with a reductio: if number were a single entity subsisting in multiple enumerated things, then impossible consequences follow. He gives two examples: (1) an existent and a non-existent cannot have a single identical subsisting entity making them two; (2) two persons in different locations cannot have a single identical meaning subsisting in them. Therefore, number is merely a mental consideration (iʿtibārī), not an external reality. The commentator clarifies that the author means number cannot subsist in enumerated objects as a real inherent quality (like blackness), but only as a mental abstraction. The commentator also notes that numbers as types have real properties, but that does not entail external existence of the number as an accident. The passage concludes that external objects are described by number, but number itself is not an external existent—only a mental consideration, and that is sufficient.

Misconception: If numbers have real properties (like squareness), they must be external existents.
Correction: Properties belong to numbers as types (species), not as accidents inhering in objects.

B) Abstractive subsistence (qiyām intizāʿī)

Key Takeaway
The commentary establishes that number is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī) that can describe external objects without being an external existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How might this awareness change the way you pursue or fear it?
?
The passage states that numbers as species have real properties, but number as an accident is mental. How does this distinction help you avoid confusing your own mental models of reality with reality itself? Can you recall a time when you argued about something as if your mental model were identical to the external thing, causing conflict or error?

How does this humbling truth press you to rely more on revelation and less on your own conceptual frameworks when approaching matters of faith?

The Denial of Magnitude by the Mutakallimun
The author explains why the mutakallimun deny magnitude as a continuous quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun reject the existence of magnitude as a continuous accident and how they explain extension as mere atomic aggregates.

Core Idea
For mutakallimun, extension is just atoms arranged, not a real continuous quantity.

The mutakallimun's atomism leads them to deny magnitude as a real accident. This passage explains their reasoning: since bodies are composed of indivisible parts that are actually separate, there is no continuous connection. Instead, what we call lines, surfaces, and bodies are merely conventional names for atomic arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the denial
Start by reading the Arabic text and translation. Notice the opening claim that mutakallimun denied magnitude just as they denied number.
2
Identify the atomistic premise
Focus on the argument that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (juzʾ lā yatajazzaʾ) and that these parts are actually separate, with only imperceptible junctions. This is the basis for denying continuous connection.
3
Understand the alternative explanation
See how mutakallimun explain the appearance of extension: atoms arranged in one direction form a substantial line, in two directions a substantial surface, in three a body. These are not real accidents but conventional aggregates.
Contrast with the philosophers' view of continuous quantity as an accident.

[ The Sixth Discourse: They Denied Magnitude ] (They) i.e., the mutakallimun (denied magnitude just as they denied number), based on the fact that the composition of the body according to them (is from the indivisible part, as will come), (for the connection between the parts) from which the body is composed (according to them) is non-existent; rather they are actually separate, but one does not perceive their separation due to the smallness of the junctions upon which the parts come into contact. And if the matter is so, (then how can it be conceded by them that there exists) in the body (a connection) i.e., a continuous thing in itself having a state in the body, (and that the parts) which are supposed in the body (have a common boundary) like magnitudes and their loci? Rather, if the body is composed of indivisible parts, the existence of any magnitude is not established, for there are only atomic substances (jawāhir farda). So if they are arranged in one direction, there results from them something divisible in one dimension, which some call a substantial line (khaṭṭ jawharī). If they are arranged in two directions, there results something divisible in two dimensions, and it may be called a substantial surface (saṭḥ jawharī). If they are arranged in three dimensions, there results what is called a body (jism) conventionally. So the line is part of the surface, and the surface is part of the body. Thus we have only the body and its parts, all of which are of the category of substances (jawāhir), so there is no existence for magnitude that is an accident (ʿaraḍ), whether line, surface, or mathematical body, as the philosophers claimed. Then he began to... Commentary: (And that the parts which are supposed, etc.) It is not hidden from you that the meaning of the connection of the body according to the philosophers is its being a locus for continuous quantity, not that there exists between its parts a common boundary, for that entails a part and what is in its ruling. So the correct thing is to say: And that the parts which are supposed in magnitude have a common boundary between them, and his statement should be left as in 'like magnitudes and their loci'. Commentary: (Some call it, i.e., the mutakallimun, and they are the Muʿtazila, for they stipulated three dimensions for the body; as for the Ashʿarites, they say: whatever is composed of two or more parts is a body.) Commentary: (Then he began, etc.) Apparently it should be said: It is an explanation of the reason for difference in the small and large. Commentary: (i.e., a suppositional and mental consideration; he meant that its essence is suppositional, not existing externally, even if the description of its locus by it is real.) Commentary: (Some call it a substantial line) and some call the composite of two or more parts a body.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's denial of magnitude (al-miqdār) as a continuous quantity. The argument is based on their doctrine of atomism: bodies are composed of indivisible parts (al-juzʾ alladhī lā yatajazzaʾ). There is no real connection between these parts; they are actually separate, though their separation is imperceptible due to small junctions. Therefore, it cannot be conceded that there is a continuous thing (ittiṣāl) in the body or that the parts share a common boundary (ḥadd mushtarak). If the body is only atoms, then the only things that exist are the individual atoms. Their arrangement in one direction yields a 'substantial line', in two directions a 'substantial surface', and in three dimensions a 'body' — but these are merely conventional names for aggregates, not real continuous magnitudes. Thus, there is no accident of magnitude (line, surface, or mathematical body) as the philosophers claim. The commentator clarifies that the philosophers' concept of connection (ittiṣāl) is different: they mean that the body is a locus for continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttaṣil), not that there is a common boundary between parts. The commentator also notes that the Muʿtazila require three dimensions for a body, while the Ashʿarites define a body as a composite of two or more parts. The final comment identifies that 'substantial line' is a term used by some mutakallimun.

Correction: They only deny it as a real accidental attribute, affirming merely conventional lines and surfaces made of atoms.

Does this truth change how you trust in the physical world? Will you commit today to reciting 'Hasbi Allah' whenever you feel overly reliant on material means?

Do you find yourself leaning toward a view of the world that diminishes God's direct control? Why Mutakallimūn Deny Continuous Quantity
Al-Ījī states the denial of quantity and explains atomist reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimūn denied continuous quantity (al-miqdār) and how they explained the appearance of lines, surfaces, and bodies as arrangements of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
For the mutakallimūn, bodies are just atoms arranged in space—there is no continuous quantity.

Al-Ījī presents a core doctrine of the mutakallimūn: the denial of continuous quantity. This is based on their atomistic physics, where bodies are composed of indivisible atoms that are actually separate but appear connected. In this passage, he explains the reasoning and offers an alternative account of lines, surfaces, and bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
2
Understand the atomist premise
The mutakallimūn hold that bodies are made of indivisible parts (jawhar fard) that are actually separate, with no real connection.
This contradicts the common-sense view of continuous bodies.
3
See how this denies continuous quantity
Since there is no real connection, there can be no continuous quantity (miqdār) inhering in the body. The quantity that philosophers speak of does not exist.
4
Learn the alternative explanation
When atoms are arranged in one, two, or three directions, they form what are called substantial line, surface, or body—but these are not accidents, just arrangements.
This answers the objection that bodies have size and shape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السادس : إنهم أنكروا المقدار ( إنهم ) أي المتكلمين ( أنكروا المقدار كما أنكروا العدد ( بناء على أن تركيب الجسم عندهم ( من الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ كما سيأتي ( فإنه الاتصال بين الأجزاء ) التي تركب الجسم منها ( عندهم ) بل هي منفصلة بالحقيقة إلا أنه لا يحس بانفصالها لصغر المفاصل التي تماست الأجزاء عليها وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ( فكيف يسلم عندهم ( أن ثمة) أي في الجسم (اتصالاً ) أي أمراً متصلاً في حد ذاته ذو عرض حال في الجسم ( وأنَّ الأجزاء) التي تفرض في الجسم ( بينها حد مشترك ) كما المقادير ومحالها بل إذا كان الجسم مركباً من أجزاء لا تتجزأ لم يثبت وجود شيء من المقادير إذ ليس هناك إلا الجواهر الفردة فإذا انتظمت في سمت واحد حصل منها أمر منقسم في وجهة واحدة يسميه بعضهم خطاً جوهرياً، وإذا انتظمت في سمتين حصل أمر منقسم في جهتين، وقد يسمى سطحاً جوهرياً، وإذا انتظمت في الجهات الثلاث حصل ما يسمى جسماً اتفاقاً. فالخط جزء من السطح، والسطح جزء من الجسم فليس لنا إلا الجسم وأجزاؤه وكلها من قبيل الجواهر فلا وجود لمقدار هو عرض إما خط أو سطح أو جسم تعليمي كما زعمت الفلاسفة.
The second observation - the sixth topic: That they denied quantity. (They) i.e. the mutakallimūn (denied quantity just as they denied number), based on the fact that the composition of the body according to them (is from the indivisible part as will come), for the connection between the parts from which the body is composed (according to them) is actually separate in reality, except that its separation is not perceived due to the smallness of the joints on which the parts meet. And if the matter is so, then how can it be accepted by them that there is in the body a connection, a continuous thing in itself having a width, and that the parts supposed in the body have a common boundary as in quantities and their places? Rather, if the body is composed of indivisible parts, the existence of something of the quantities is not established, for there are only the individual atoms. If they are arranged in one direction, there results from them something divisible in one direction which some call a substantial line; if arranged in two directions, there results something divisible in two directions, and it may be called a substantial surface; if arranged in the three directions, there results what is called a body by convention. So the line is part of the surface, and the surface is part of the body, and we have only the body and its parts, all of which are of the type of substances; so there is no existence of a quantity that is an accident, either line or surface or mathematical body, as the philosophers claimed.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the position of the mutakallimūn (theologians) regarding the denial of quantity (al-miqdār) and explains the reasoning based on their atomistic theory of body composition. The passage begins by stating that the mutakallimūn denied quantity just as they denied number. The reason given is that their understanding of the composition of a body is from indivisible parts (atoms). According to them, the supposed connection between the parts of the body is not real; rather, the parts are actually separate, but we do not perceive the separation because the joints where the parts meet are extremely small. Given this lack of real connection, the passage asks how it can be accepted that there is any continuous thing in the body, or that the supposed parts share a common boundary as in quantities. Since the body is composed of indivisible atoms, no continuous quantity (muttasil) exists. What exists are only individual atoms (jawāhir farda). When these atoms are arranged in a line, they form a 'substantial line' (khaṭṭ jawharī); when arranged in two dimensions, a 'substantial surface' (saṭḥ jawharī); when in three dimensions, a 'body' by convention. Hence, the only existents are the body and its atomic parts, all of which are substances, not accidents. Therefore, there is no quantity that is an accident (such as a line, surface, or mathematical body) as the philosophers (falāsifa) claimed. The passage thus establishes the position of the mutakallimūn that continuous quantity is not a real accident inhering in bodies, but is merely an arrangement of atoms.

Explaining the Three Properties of Quantity Without Continuous Quantity
The commentator explains how difference, division, and count can be accounted for on the atomic hypothesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimūn account for difference, division, and counting in bodies without assuming a continuous quantity, thus defending the atomic hypothesis.

Core Idea
Difference, division, and counting do not prove continuous quantity; atoms suffice.

The philosophers argued that three properties—difference in size, divisibility, and countability—prove the existence of a continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil). In this passage, the commentator refutes this by showing that these properties can be explained on the atomic hypothesis. He explains each property in turn, demonstrating that the atomists' position is consistent and that any appearance of continuity is due to the imagination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get the overall argument. Notice the three properties discussed.
The passage begins with a refutation of the philosophers.
2
Identify the three properties
The passage lists difference (tafāwut), division (qisma), and counting (‘ād). For each, the commentator shows how they occur without continuous quantity.
3
Examine the explanation for each property
For difference: due to number of atoms and strength of connection. For division: mental supposition of one atom without another. For counting: by individual atoms; imagined continuity is rejected.
Pay attention to the phrase 'except by imagination' and its rejection.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that these three properties cannot prove the existence of a continuous quantity subsisting in the body.
5
Review the gloss on 'supposing an atom without an atom'
The gloss clarifies that mental supposition corresponds to real distinctness of atoms.
This supports the atomic view.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Acceptance of division and existence of the counter according to the partisans of the part, in refutation of what the philosophers said that the three things are properties of quantity. Then he began to point out the three mentioned properties of quantity, and how they are conceived in the body on the assumption that it is composed of individual atoms. So he said: (And the difference) between bodies in smallness and bigness, increase and decrease, (goes back to the paucity and abundance of parts; what has fewer parts is smaller in size and less. And difference may occur due to the strength of connection of the parts and the existence of gaps between them, so it is permissible that the body be described with equality and inequality without there being a continuous quantity called a measure. And (the division) that is hypothetical that occurs to the body on that assumption (its meaning is supposing an atom without an atom), for each one of them is a thing different from the other, so it is valid for the body to accept division without a continuous quantity subsisting in it. (And it has no counter other than the parts), i.e. it is permissible that the body be counted by each one of the individual atoms that are its parts, and there is no other thing by which to count it as one continuous volume at all. (Except by imagination), for sometimes one imagines and supposes in it something of that continuous such that he counts it, and he imagines that there is a quantity that is a continuous quantity in which a unit counter can be supposed. (And its judgment is rejected) because it arises from the lack of perception of the joints and separation due to the inability of the sense to perceive the details of very small things. So counting in the body occurred without a continuous quantity. And by what we have mentioned, it is revealed that it is not possible to infer from the establishment of any of these three things in the body the existence of a quantity subsisting in it. His statement: (supposing an atom without an atom) 'without' is in the accusative as a circumstantial modifier, meaning surpassing an atom; the gist is supposing two atoms in it as a supposition corresponding to reality.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of quantity by addressing the three properties that the philosophers used as evidence for continuous quantity: difference (al-tafāwut), division (al-qisma), and counting (al-‘ād). The commentator aims to show that these three can be explained without positing a continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil), thus supporting the atomic view. First, the passage mentions that the proponents of the atom (aṣḥāb al-juz’) accept the reality of division and counting as a refutation of the philosophers who claimed these three are properties of continuous quantity. Then, the commentator explains each property: 1. Difference in size (smallness, largeness, increase, decrease) is due to the number of atoms: fewer atoms means smaller size. Also, differences can occur due to the strength of connection or gaps, without a continuous quantity. 2. Division (al-qisma) is understood as mentally supposing one atom separate from another, not a real division of a continuum. Since each atom is distinct, the body is divisible in thought without a continuous substratum. 3. Counting (al-‘ād) is done by counting the individual atoms; there is no separate continuous unit to count. If one imagines a continuous whole, that is a product of the imagination (al-wahm) because the senses fail to perceive the small joints, so one mistakenly thinks there is a continuum. This imagined judgment is rejected (ḥukmuhu mardūd) because it arises from sensory limitation. The passage concludes that these three features cannot prove the existence of a continuous quantity. Then a gloss clarifies the phrase 'supposing an atom without an atom' – it means mentally positing two atoms as distinct, which corresponds to real distinctness. Thus, the passage defends the atomistic position against the philosophers' arguments from the three properties.

القسمة

الوهم
الجوهر الفرد

Correction: The passage shows they use the number of atoms and strength of connection to explain size differences.

Checkpoint

B) By the strength of the connection only.
C) By both the number of atoms and the strength of connection.
D) By the shape of the atoms.

Key Takeaway
Difference, division, and counting in bodies are explained by the number and arrangement of atoms, not by a continuous quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your senses every day to judge size, division, and count. The passage shows that your senses can be deceived into imagining continuity where there is only discreteness. When has your perception of something being 'one whole' blinded you to the many small components that make it up? How can you train yourself to see the discrete parts in your life—your actions, your habits, your relationships—and not be fooled by an illusion of unity?
?
The philosophers held a common-sense view that continuous quantity is obvious. The atomists challenge that by offering a deeper explanation. What is one belief you hold about yourself or the world that you consider obvious, but might be merely a product of limited perception? Are you willing to subject that belief to the same rigorous scrutiny the mutakallimūn apply to the philosophers?
?
Imagination (al-wahm) can lead to false judgments, as when one imagines continuous quantity. In your daily life, where does your imagination fill in gaps that your senses cannot perceive, and lead you to conclusions not based on reality? How can you learn to pause and distinguish between what is real and what is just a product of your mind?

The Philosophers' First Proof for Continuous Quantity (The Candle Example)
Al-Ījī presents the philosophers' first proof for continuous quantity from the same body receiving different quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers use the example of a single body (like a candle) to prove that continuous quantity is an accident distinct from the body.

Core Idea
A single body can take on different shapes—quantity must be an accident.

The philosophers argue that when the same body (like a candle) is made into different sizes and shapes, the body itself remains unchanged. This shows that the varying dimensions are not the body itself but an additional quality—continuous quantity. This passage presents their first proof and addresses a potential charge of circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the structure: a claim, an example, and then clarifications from the commentator.
Pay attention to the specific example of the candle changing dimensions.
2
Understand the key observation
The same body receives different quantities while remaining essentially the same body (specific bodyness preserved). This is the crux of the proof.
3
Identify the conclusion
Therefore, quantity is an accident distinct from the body, called the 'mathematical body' (al-jism al-ta‘līmī).
The passage identifies these quantities as continuous and extending in three dimensions.
4
Consider the objections and replies
The commentator clarifies that 'quantities' here mean conventional measurements, so there is no circularity. Also, the preservation of bodyness is assumed.
This addresses a common misconception.
5
Reflect on the significance
This proof establishes quantity as a real accident, foundational to later discussions in kalam and philosophy.
Think about why this matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الحكماء في إثباته بوجهين: الأول أن الجسم الواحد كالشمعة مثلاً تتوارد عليه مقادير مختلفة فتارة يجعل طوله شبراً وعرضه ذراعاً وتارة بالعكس وتارة مدوراً وتارة مكعبا وهو ما يحيط به سطوح ستة هي مربعات متساوية وحينئذ فقد توارد عليه مقادير مختلفة مع بقاء جسميته المخصوصة ما لم يطرأ عليه انفصال، وتلك المقادير المختلفة كميات سارية فيه ممتدة في الجهات الثلاث وهي الجسم التعليمي (لا). قوله : ( مقادير) بالمعنى اللغوي أعني المقادير المحسوسة فلا يتوهم المصادرة . قوله : (مع بقاء جسميته المخصوصة) هذا إنما يتم لو لم تكن المقدار من مخصصات الجسمي، وهو ممنوع إلى أن يقوم الدليل عليه . قوله: (تتوارد عليه مقادير (مختلفة المراد بالمقادير هاهنا هو المقادير المتعارفة التي ينكرها أحد وكذا المراد بالسطح فيما سيأتي فلا يرد أن فيه مصادرة لتوقفه على ثبوت المقادير.
And the philosophers have argued in proving it by two proofs: The first is that a single body, like a candle for example, receives different quantities. Sometimes its length is made a span and its width a cubit, sometimes the opposite, sometimes round, sometimes cubic—what is surrounded by six squared surfaces. And then it has received different quantities while its specific bodyness remains as long as no separation occurs to it. And those different quantities are continuous quantities permeating it, extending in the three dimensions, and it is the mathematical body (not...). His statement: (quantities) in the linguistic sense, I mean the sensible measurements, so no circularity is imagined. His statement: (with the preservation of its specific bodyness) This only holds if the quantity is not among the particularizers of bodyness, which is prevented until proof is established for it. His statement: (receives different quantities) What is meant by 'quantities' here is the conventional measurements that no one denies, and likewise what is meant by 'surface' in what follows, so it is not objected that there is circularity since it depends on the proof of quantities.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of the philosophers (al-ḥukamā’) for the existence of continuous quantity (al-miqdār) as an accident inhering in the body. The proof uses the example of a single body, such as a candle, which can be assigned different dimensions: one time its length is a span and its width a cubit, another time the reverse, or it can be made round or cubic, while the body itself remains the same and does not undergo separation. Since these different measurements apply to the same body, they cannot be identical with the body; rather, they must be distinct quantities that inhere in the body as accidents. These quantities are continuous (sāriya) and extend in the three dimensions, constituting what is called the 'mathematical body' (al-jism al-ta‘līmī). The passage then includes the commentator's clarifications: first, that 'quantities' here mean sensible measurements, so the argument does not beg the question; second, that the preservation of specific bodyness is only valid if quantity is not considered a differentiator of bodies (which is disputed); third, that the quantities mentioned are conventional and not the specific continuous quantity being proven, so there is no circularity. Thus, the proof aims to show that quantity is an accident distinct from the body, but the commentator hints at objections.

Key Terms
الجسم التعليمي
الكم المتصل
المقادير المتعارفة
مخصصات الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it uses 'quantities' to prove 'quantity'.
Correction: The commentator explains that 'quantities' in the premise refer to conventional sensible measurements, not the specific continuous quantity being proven, so the argument is not circular.

Checkpoint
In the philosophers' first proof, what is the key observation about the candle that is supposed to demonstrate continuous quantity?
A) The candle changes color and texture.
B) The candle can have different dimensions while remaining the same body.
C) The candle melts and then solidifies into a different shape.
D) The candle is divisible into parts.

Key Takeaway
The candle example demonstrates that continuous quantity is a real accident distinct from the body, as shown by the same body taking on different dimensions while remaining essentially unchanged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that a single body can receive different quantities while remaining the same specific body. Now think: what is the 'specific bodyness' of your own self that remains despite all the changes in your life—your age, your health, your possessions? Have you been confusing the changing quantities of your life with your unchanging soul? How will you remind yourself daily of this distinction?
?
The commentator points out that the proof's premise is not yet established. In your own faith, do you ever accept an argument without examining its hidden assumptions? What is one area of your belief where you need to check if you are building on unproven premises?
?
If a candle can be reshaped so easily, what does that tell you about the fragility of this world? Are you investing your heart in things that can be reshaped and changed, or in the One who never changes? What concrete step will you take today to shift your attachment from the changeable to the Eternal?
